</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 4.</span> <p id="p-37" class="first-child">PSGs involved in the predicted integrated pathways of rice flower development. Within the genes assigned to categories of “flower development” (GO: 0009908) and “pollination” (GO: 0009856), the 14 genes that are homologs to reproduction-related genes or associated with the flowering process are shown with evidence of positive selection (in red boxes). <em>ASHH2, ABSENT</em>, <em>SMALL</em>, <em>OR HOMEOTIC DISCS 1 HOMOLOG 2</em>; <em>CRY2</em>, <em>CRYPTOCHROME 2</em>; <em>HEN1</em>, <em>HUA ENHANCER 1</em>; le, lemma; lo, lodicule; <em>LHY</em>, <em>LATE ELONGATED HYPOCOTYL</em>; <em>MOS3/SAR3</em>, <em>MODIFIER OF SNC1</em>,<em>3</em>/<em>SUPPRESSOR OF AUXIN RESISTANCE 3</em>; pa, palea; <em>PDIL</em>, <em>PROTEIN DISULFIDE ISOMERASE</em>-<em>LIKE</em>; <em>PEP</em>, <em>PEPPER</em>; <em>PFT1</em>, <em>PHYTOCHROME AND FLOWERING TIME 1</em>; <em>PG</em>, <em>POLYGALACTURONASE</em>; pi, pistil; <em>SEC5</em>, <em>EXOCYST COMPLEX COMPONENT</em>; st, stamen; <em>TAF6</em>, <em>TATA BOX BINDING PROTEIN</em><em>-ASSOCIATED FACTOR 6</em>; <em>TMS1</em>, <em>THERMOSENSITIVE MALE STERILE 1</em>; <em>VIL1</em>, <em>VERNALIZATION INSENSITIVE 3</em>-<em>LIKE 1</em>; <em>XTH</em>, <em>XYLOGLUCAN ENDOTRANSGLUCOSYLASE/HYDROLASE</em>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Noncoding RNA Gene Family Evolution.</h3><p id="p-38">Using the obtained ncRNA gene annotations (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S13</a>), comparisons of ncRNA gene families showed that they varied in number across the phylogeny of the six rice species, although these changes were not generally dramatic (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S6</a>). GO analysis of predicted miRNA target genes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S43</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sd01.xls" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>), most of which were verified by RNA-Seq data (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Tables S9 and S10</a>), indicates that the majority of miRNA target genes were conserved in a wide range of important functional categories across all species (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S31</a>). Several biological processes or molecular functions were significantly enriched in some target genes of all six rice species (<em>P</em> &lt; 0.05), such as death (GO: 0016265), cellular process (GO: 0009987), and response to stimulus (GO: 0050896). However, some GO terms, for example, cellular component organization (GO: 0016043), macromolecular complex (GO: 0032991), organelle (GO: 0043226), and organelle part (GO: 0044422), were only enriched in MER (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S32</a>). Comparative sequence analysis of all classes of ncRNA genes across the six rice species indicated that snRNA genes exhibit relatively high nucleotide substitution rates (2.61%) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S33 and Table S44</a>). Individual species-based analyses confirmed the overall conserved pattern of sequence evolution for every class of ncRNA genes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S34</a>), but we did observe that, except for some housekeeping ncRNA genes (tRNAs, snoRNAs, and snRNAs), divergence rates of pre-miRNAs, mature miRNAs, and miRNA targets in the two cultivated rice species, consistent with genomic background components, were comparatively lower than in their counterpart wild progenitors, probably resulting from bottleneck effects during the domestication of cultivated rice. Among the six AA-genome species, MER exhibited the highest rates of sequence evolution for all ncRNA genes and genomic background components.</p><p id="p-39">MiRNA genes have emerged as master regulators of plant growth, particularly in plant development processes and a variety of stress responses (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>). Comparisons of nucleotide substitutions in miRNA genes and target sites across the six rice species showed that most were conserved without mutations, whereas a small proportion (98 of 227 in miRNA and 560 of 1,598 in targets) of them were variable for one or more nucleotide substitutions (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S35</a>). A systematic survey of the 21 miRNA gene families related to flower development indicated a wide range of variation in copy number among the six <em>Oryza</em> species (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S45</a>). In addition to the expansion of the MADS-box gene family (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S32</a>), copy number variation in several miRNA gene families may partially account for the characteristic differences of flower development and reproduction, providing new resources for studying regulation by miRNA genes. Random sampling of four miRNA genes (osa-miR159a, osa-miR159e, osa-miR159f, and osa-miR1847), belonging to two miRNA gene families (miRNA159, miRNA1847), uncovered differential amplification in one or more of the other five non-SAT species. In addition to functional enrichment of protein phosphorylation (GO: 0006468) of the lineage-specific gene families in SAT and NIV (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sd04.xls" class="in-nw">Dataset S4</a>), the detected nucleotide mutations in certain lineages may be responsible for phenotypic variation and different ecological adaptations, such as tolerance of drought conditions and phosphate starvation (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S37 and S38 and Table S43</a>).</p></div></div><div class="section" id="sec-8"><h2>Concluding Remarks</h2><p id="p-40" class="flushleft">We present high-quality reference genome sequences for <em>O. nivara</em>, <em>O. glaberrima</em>, <em>O. barthii</em>, <em>O. glumapatula</em>, and <em>O. meridionalis</em>. Over the past century, the introduction of genes from AA-genome relatives into Asian cultivated rice has helped expand rice genetic diversity important to the generation of environmentally resilient and high-yielding varieties. This approach led to the discovery of the “wild-abortive rice” genes from <em>O. rufipogon</em>, which allowed the facile production of hybrid rice, a landmark in rice improvement (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>). Similarly, we expect that the genome sequences described in this article will become valuable resources for more extensive exploitation of wild <em>Oryza</em> germplasms to enhance rice breeding programs. With the high-quality sequence of the Nipponbare genome of <em>O. sativa</em> as a guide, our multispecies comparisons of the five AA rice genomes, aided by the recently reported FF genome of <em>O. brachyantha</em> as an outgroup (<a id="xref-ref-23-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>), afford novel opportunities for addressing a wealth of questions about gene and genome evolution in rice. The use of multispecies orthology and careful annotations of functional features provide convincing evidence in strong support of gene models, including both protein-coding and ncRNA genes.</p><p id="p-41">Overall genome size, number of genes, contents of TEs, and genomic architecture and gene colinearity are all fairly conserved across these rice genomes. However, MER is an outlier by several criteria, including evidence for greater divergence at levels of protein-coding genes, ncRNA genes, and structural variation, plus accelerated molecular evolution and specific functional enrichment of miRNA target genes. This greater divergence in MER is at least partly caused by the fact that it is more than 2.5-fold more distantly related to the other AA genomes studied than they are to each other, but it may also be an outcome of unique environmental demands for MER adaptation in its Australian home.</p><p id="p-42">Abundant microstructural rearrangements and lineage-specific gene gains and/or losses are expected to be largely responsible for the functional dynamics of adaptation over evolutionary time in these closely related rice species. This expectation is particularly well-exemplified in the <em>PROG1</em>-orthologous regions, where numerous cases of large-scale SVs were observed and, for example, the <em>PROG1</em>-encoding gene was deleted in several species, thereby leading to their current erect growth habit. Segmental duplications that are abundant across these <em>Oryza</em> genomes have played an important role in driving rice genome evolution. The genes within these duplications were significantly enriched for important biological functions, thus providing essential sources of the gene dosage variation that can permit subfunctionalization or neofunctionalization.</p><p id="p-43">The rapid evolution of gene families, particularly a sizeable fraction showing fast and/or lineage-specific expansions and contractions, exhibiting different functional enrichment, is also expected to contribute to the adaptive evolution of <em>Oryza</em> species to globally diverse habitats. We detected a considerable portion of lineage-specific protein-coding genes under positive selection that were involved in flower development, reproduction, and biotic- and abiotic defenses. These genes are particularly good candidates to investigate for adaptive evolution to changing environments. Future studies should use advanced genetic tools (e.g., gene replacement and other forms of reverse genetics) to determine which of these genes are involved in which adaptations. Significant GO enrichment of defense-related genes was detected in the five non-SAT species, indicating that AA-genome relatives of SAT serve as reservoirs for novel disease resistance alleles that may enhance rice breeding programs.</p><p id="p-44">Because these comparative genomic analyses were empowered by being embedded in the context of a robust phylogeny, we were able to discover that the African branch (GLA/BAR) differs in several evolutionary behaviors from the Asian branch (SAT/NIV). The African branch exhibits one-third fewer genomic SVs; remarkable evidence for positive selection with distinctly lowered levels of both <em>dN</em> and <em>dS</em> values; more gene loss (<em>PROG1</em> and <em>S5</em>); and larger numbers of PSGs significantly overrepresented in categories mainly related to flower development, reproduction, and defense processes than the Asian branch. This finding indicates accelerated molecular evolution after their very recent speciation to adapt quickly to specific environments in Africa. Comparative analysis also found some instances where both SAT and GLA diverged in similar ways from their counterpart wild progenitors NIV and BAR, as shown by significantly more expansions for all top 10 gene families of reproductive and ecological significance and lowered sequence evolutionary rates of ncRNA genes in the domesticated lineages. Considering the quick extinction and endangered status of natural populations due to severe deforestation in worldwide tropical and subtropical regions (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>), it is our hope that the genome assemblies and genomic variation data presented here will provide valuable information to aid the global conservation of these precious wild rice species.</p></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-9"><h2>Methods</h2><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Sequencing, Assembly, and Validation.</h3><p id="p-45">We sequenced the five AA-genome <em>Oryza</em> species by using a WGS strategy with next-generation sequencing technologies from paired-end, small-insert libraries (∼300 bp and ∼500 bp) and mate-pair, large-insert libraries (2 kb, 4 kb, 6 kb, and 8 kb). The whole-genome de novo assemblies were built using SOAPdenovo (<a id="xref-ref-22-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>). To assess the quality of the assembled genomes, we used three evaluation approaches. First, we separately mapped our assembled genome sequences (NIV, GLA, BAR, GLU, and MER) to the SAT genome to estimate the coverage of the assemblies using MUMmer (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). We further compared orthologous genomic segments based on the aligned orthology map of the six AA-genome <em>Oryza</em> species (SAT, NIV, GLA, BAR, GLU, and MER) and calculated the coverage of the gene-containing genomic regions corresponding to the SAT genome for each species. We next evaluated the assembly quality by using Nipponbare gene models to examine whether they are split into multiple contigs into the assemblies. Second, we took the four species (NIV, GLA, GLU, and MER) with BAC end sequencing (BES) data from OMAP and aligned these BES data with our de novo assembled genomes. Of these species, BES datasets for NIV, GLA, and BAR were separately mapped to our de novo assembled scaffolds before the de novo assemblies were improved by BES integration. Finally, by running both MUMmer (<a id="xref-ref-53-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>) and Blastz (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>), the assembly quality was further evaluated by aligning our assembled genomes against sequences from the short arms of chromosome 3 released by the OMAP.</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Annotation.</h3><p id="p-46">Putative protein-coding loci were identified based on ab initio annotation, homology-based gene prediction, and EST-aided annotation, and quality validation of the gene model was performed by using transcriptome, EST, and homologous peptide evidence. De novo searches for and annotation of TEs were completed by integrating RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org" class="in-nw">www.repeatmasker.org</a>), LTR_STRUCT (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>), RECON (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>), LTR_Finder (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>), and RepeatScout (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">58</a>). The five different types of ncRNA genes, including tRNA, rRNA, snoRNA, snRNA, and miRNA genes, were predicted using de novo and homology search methods. We annotated miRNAs using two steps. First, we downloaded the existing rice miRNA entries from miRBase, release 18.0 (<a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">59</a>). Then, the conserved miRNAs were identified by mapping all miRBase-recorded SAT miRNA precursor sequences against the assembled NIV, GLA, BAR, GLU, and MER genomes using nucleotide–nucleotide BLAST (blastn) with cutoffs at an E value of &lt;1e<sup>−5</sup>, identity of &gt;80%, and query coverage of &gt;80%. Second, additional miRNA genes were identified by aligning all miRBase-recorded grass miRNA precursor sequences against our assembled genomes using blastn with cutoffs at an E value of &lt;1e<sup>−5</sup>, identity of &gt;60%, and query coverage of &gt;60%, because deep-sequencing genome projects have found numerous new miRNAs in grasses. When a miRNA was mapped to a target <em>Oryza</em> genome, flanking sequences were next checked for hairpin structures. Those loci that fulfilled miRNA precursor secondary structures were annotated as additional miRNA genes. We excluded miRNA genes that identified multiple hits, most likely repeat sequences, in the six assembled rice genomes.</p><p id="p-47">More methods and details of data collection are provided in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1418307111/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1418307111.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>.</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-48">We thank the International Rice Research Institute (Manila, Philippines) for providing rice germplasm. This work was supported by the Top Talents Program of Yunnan Province (Grant 20080A009), the Hundreds Oversea Talents Program of Yunnan Province, the project of the Yunnan Innovation Team, the Key Project of the Natural Science Foundation of Yunnan Province (Grant 2010CC011), the Hundreds Talents Program of the Chinese Academy of Sciences (CAS), a grant from the CAS (Grant KSCX2-YW-N-029), a grant from the Chinese Department of Science and Technology (973 Program Grant 2007CB815703), and a startup grant from the Kunming Institute of Botany, CAS (to L.-Z.G.).</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="fn-equal" id="fn-4"><p id="p-4"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-4-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>Q.-J.Z., T.Z., E.-H.X., C.S., Y.-L.L., Y.Z., and Y.L. contributed equally to this work.</p></li><li class="corresp" id="corresp-1"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-corresp-1-1">↵</a><sup>2</sup>To whom correspondence may be addressed.         <h2 class="pane-title"><span class="pane-title-text">You May Also be Interested in</span></h2>
    
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="view view-pnas-featured-items-article-rhs-nodequeue view-id-pnas_featured_items_article_rhs_nodequeue view-display-id-panel_pane pnas-featured-item-list pnas-featured-item-list-rhs view-dom-id-3b47a212120bbf6d33df83782bbefb16">
        
  
  
      <div class="view-content">
        <div class="views-row-odd views-row-first">
    <article class="node-view-teaser node-featured-item">

    <div class="node--image">
    <a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/118/4/e2025750118" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="field field-name-field-featured-item-image field-type-image field-label-hidden"><div class="field-items"><div class="field-item even"><img src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/styles/medium/public/additional-assets/featured-item/sc-220.png?itok=T8aPEV0g" width="220" height="220" alt="Abstract depiction of a guitar and musical note" /></div></div></div></a>  </div> <!-- End of image -->
  
  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/118/4/e2025750118" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Science & Culture: At the nexus of music and medicine, some see disease treatments</a></div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__description">Although the evidence is still limited, a growing body of research suggests music may have beneficial effects for diseases such as Parkinson’s.</div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__credits text-small">Image credit: Shutterstock/agsandrew.</div>
    
    
  </div> <!-- End of body -->

</article>
  </div>
  <div class="views-row-even">
    <article class="node-view-teaser node-featured-item">

    <div class="node--image">
    <a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/118/3/e2025207118" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="field field-name-field-featured-item-image field-type-image field-label-hidden"><div class="field-items"><div class="field-item even"><img src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/styles/medium/public/additional-assets/featured-item/o-220.png?itok=2mX2CLo4" width="220" height="220" alt="Scientist looking at an electronic tablet" /></div></div></div></a>  </div> <!-- End of image -->
  
  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/118/3/e2025207118" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Opinion: Standardizing gene product nomenclature—a call to action</a></div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__description">Biomedical communities and journals need to standardize nomenclature of gene products to enhance accuracy in scientific and public communication.</div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__credits text-small">Image credit: Shutterstock/greenbutterfly.</div>
    
    
  </div> <!-- End of body -->

</article>
  </div>
  <div class="views-row-odd">
    <article class="node-view-teaser node-featured-item">

    <div class="node--image">
    <a href="http://blog.pnas.org/2021/01/study-reveals-evolutionary-origins-of-fold-switching-protein/" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="field field-name-field-featured-item-image field-type-image field-label-hidden"><div class="field-items"><div class="field-item even"><img src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/styles/medium/public/additional-assets/featured-item/jcb-220.png?itok=gSEyfdzb" width="220" height="220" alt="One red and one yellow modeled protein structures" /></div></div></div></a>  </div> <!-- End of image -->
  
  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="http://blog.pnas.org/2021/01/study-reveals-evolutionary-origins-of-fold-switching-protein/" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Journal Club: Study reveals evolutionary origins of fold-switching protein</a></div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__description">Shapeshifting designs could have wide-ranging pharmaceutical and biomedical applications in coming years.</div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__credits text-small">Image credit: Acacia Dishman/Medical College of Wisconsin.</div>
    
    
  </div> <!-- End of body -->

</article>
  </div>
  <div class="views-row-even">
    <article class="node-view-teaser node-featured-item">

    <div class="node--image">
    <a href="https://traffic.libsyn.com/secure/pnas-science-sessions-podcast/amandaRodewaldIvanRudikCatherineKlingPodcast.mp3" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="field field-name-field-featured-item-image field-type-image field-label-hidden"><div class="field-items"><div class="field-item even"><img src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/styles/medium/public/additional-assets/featured-item/podcast-2021-01-04-220.png?itok=7WJRcWOH" width="218" height="220" alt="White and blue bird" /></div></div></div></a>  </div> <!-- End of image -->
  
  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="https://traffic.libsyn.com/secure/pnas-science-sessions-podcast/amandaRodewaldIvanRudikCatherineKlingPodcast.mp3" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Hazards of ozone pollution to birds</a></div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__description text-small">Amanda Rodewald, Ivan Rudik, and Catherine Kling talk about the hazards of ozone pollution to birds.</div>
    

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__listen"><a href="https://traffic.libsyn.com/secure/pnas-science-sessions-podcast/amandaRodewaldIvanRudikCatherineKlingPodcast.mp3" target="_blank" class="button-alt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Listen</a></div>

</article>

  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/117/39/24195" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Transplantation of sperm-producing stem cells</a></div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__description">CRISPR-Cas9 gene editing can improve the effectiveness of spermatogonial stem cell transplantation in mice and livestock, a study finds.</div>
